<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome of the Native American cold hardy grapevine Vitis riparia Michx. &#8216;Manitoba 37&#8217;"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Vitis riparia, a critically important Native American grapevine species, is used globally in rootstock and scion breeding and contributed to the recovery of the French wine industry during the mid-19th century phylloxera epidemic. This species has abiotic and biotic stress tolerance and the largest natural geographic distribution of the North American grapevine species. Here we report an Illumina short-read 369X coverage, draft de novo heterozygous genome sequence of V. riparia Michx. &#8216;Manitoba 37&#8217; with the size of ~495&#8201;Mb for 69,616 scaffolds and a N50 length of 518,740&#8201;bp. Using RNAseq data, 40,019 coding sequences were predicted and annotated. Benchmarking with Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis of predicted gene models found 96% of the complete BUSCOs in this assembly. The assembly continuity and completeness were further validated using V. riparia ESTs, BACs, and three de novo transcriptome assemblies of three different V. riparia genotypes resulting in &gt;98% of respective sequences/transcripts mapping with this assembly. Alignment of the V. riparia assembly and predicted CDS with the latest V. vinifera &#8216;PN40024&#8217; CDS and genome assembly showed 99% CDS alignment and a high degree of synteny. An analysis of plant transcription factors indicates a high degree of homology with the V. vinifera transcription factors. QTL mapping to V. riparia &#8216;Manitoba 37&#8217; and V. vinifera PN40024 has identified genetic relationships to phenotypic variation between species. This assembly provides reference sequences, gene models for marker development and understanding V. riparia&#8217;s genetic contributions in grape breeding and research."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Vitis riparia, a critically important Native American grapevine species, is used globally in rootstock and scion breeding and contributed to the recovery of the French wine industry during the mid-19th century phylloxera epidemic. This species has abiotic and biotic stress tolerance and the largest natural geographic distribution of the North American grapevine species. Here we report an Illumina short-read 369X coverage, draft de novo heterozygous genome sequence of V. riparia Michx. &#8216;Manitoba 37&#8217; with the size of ~495&#8201;Mb for 69,616 scaffolds and a N50 length of 518,740&#8201;bp. Using RNAseq data, 40,019 coding sequences were predicted and annotated. Benchmarking with Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis of predicted gene models found 96% of the complete BUSCOs in this assembly. The assembly continuity and completeness were further validated using V. riparia ESTs, BACs, and three de novo transcriptome assemblies of three different V. riparia genotypes resulting in &gt;98% of respective sequences/transcripts mapping with this assembly. Alignment of the V. riparia assembly and predicted CDS with the latest V. vinifera &#8216;PN40024&#8217; CDS and genome assembly showed 99% CDS alignment and a high degree of synteny. An analysis of plant transcription factors indicates a high degree of homology with the V. vinifera transcription factors. QTL mapping to V. riparia &#8216;Manitoba 37&#8217; and V. vinifera PN40024 has identified genetic relationships to phenotypic variation between species. This assembly provides reference sequences, gene models for marker development and understanding V. riparia&#8217;s genetic contributions in grape breeding and research."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Vitis riparia</i>, a critically important Native American grapevine species, is used globally in rootstock and scion breeding and contributed to the recovery of the French wine industry during the mid-19th century phylloxera epidemic. This species has abiotic and biotic stress tolerance and the largest natural geographic distribution of the North American grapevine species. Here we report an Illumina short-read 369X coverage, draft de novo heterozygous genome sequence of <i>V. riparia</i> Michx. ‘Manitoba 37’ with the size of ~495 Mb for 69,616 scaffolds and a N50 length of 518,740 bp. Using RNAseq data, 40,019 coding sequences were predicted and annotated. Benchmarking with Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO) analysis of predicted gene models found 96% of the complete BUSCOs in this assembly. The assembly continuity and completeness were further validated using <i>V. riparia</i> ESTs, BACs, and three de novo transcriptome assemblies of three different <i>V. riparia</i> genotypes resulting in &gt;98% of respective sequences/transcripts mapping with this assembly. Alignment of the <i>V. riparia</i> assembly and predicted CDS with the latest <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ CDS and genome assembly showed 99% CDS alignment and a high degree of synteny. An analysis of plant transcription factors indicates a high degree of homology with the <i>V. vinifera</i> transcription factors. QTL mapping to <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and <i>V. vinifera</i> PN40024 has identified genetic relationships to phenotypic variation between species. This assembly provides reference sequences, gene models for marker development and understanding <i>V. riparia</i>’s genetic contributions in grape breeding and research.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Grapes (<i>Vitis</i> spp.), used for wine, juice, table grapes, raisins, and rootstocks are the most valuable fruit crop in the world. While the cultivated species <i>Vitis vinifera</i> is the predominant species used in the industry, other wild grape species are important contributors to commercial production. In particular, several North American species have been used by breeders to develop scion and rootstock cultivars that are disease, insect, and abiotic stress tolerant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Reisch, B. J. &amp; Pratt, C. in Fruit Breeding, Vine and Small Fruits, (eds Janick, J. &amp; More, J. N.) Ch. 4, Vol. 2 (John Wiley and Sons, Inc., 1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4420e473">1</a></sup>. However, evidence from ongoing genome sequencing projects in grapevine demonstrates high variability between cultivars and that the wild grapevine species remain under-sampled for genomic data. With the advent of sequencing technologies for crop development, such as genotype by sequencing (GBS) and RNase H2-dependent amplicon sequencing (rhAmpSeq), many crop breeders are adopting larger genomic studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yang et al. Next generation mapping of enological traits in an F2 interspecific grapevine hybrid family. PLoS ONE 11, e0149560 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4420e477">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zou, C. et al. Haplotyping the Vitis collinear core genome with rhAmpSeq improves marker transferability in a diverse genus. Nat. Commun. 11, 413 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4420e480">3</a></sup>. Thus, development of grape genomic resources is invaluable for ongoing crop improvement and ongoing gene annotation and gene function analyses.</p><p>Fossil evidence of <i>Vitis</i> seed from the Rocky Mountain region of the United States date back to the Plaocene era (65.5–55.8 Mya)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Gong, F., Karsai, I. &amp; Liu, Y.-S. Vitis Seeds (Vitaceae) from the late Neogene Gray fossil site, Northeastern Tennessee, U.S.A. J. Rev. Palaeobot. Palyno. 162, 71–83 (2010)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4420e490">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Graham, A. The role of land bridges, ancient environments, and migrations in the assembly of the North American flora. J. Sys. Evol. 56, 405–429 (2018)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4420e490_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Manchester, S. R. Revisions to Rowland Brown’s North American Paleocene flora. Acta Musei Nationalis Pragae, Ser. B – Historia Naturalis 70, 153–210 (2014)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4420e490_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nie, Z. L. et al. Molecular phylogeny and biogeographic diversification of Parthenocissus (Vitaceae) disjunct between Asia and North America. Am. J. Bot. 97, 1342–1353 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4420e493">7</a></sup>. Recent nuclear DNA analyses suggest that the most recent common <i>Vitis</i> ancestor for the existing global grape species originated in North America, diversifying from the rest of Vitaceae ~28 Mya (CI 41.2, 16.2 Mya), with <i>Vitis</i> and <i>Muscadinia</i> diverging ~18 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wan, Y. Z. et al. A phylogenetic analysis of the grape genus (Vitis L.) reveals broad reticulation and concurrent diversification during Neogene and Quaternary climate change. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 141 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4420e507">8</a></sup>. The <i>Vitis</i> genus, containing about 60 inter-fertile living species, is suggested to have diversified at 12–1.3 Mya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wan, Y. Z. et al. A phylogenetic analysis of the grape genus (Vitis L.) reveals broad reticulation and concurrent diversification during Neogene and Quaternary climate change. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 141 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4420e514">8</a></sup>. The major cultivated species, <i>V. vinifera</i> is thought to have been domesticated from its wild ancestor <i>V. sylvestris</i> in the Mediterranean region of South and East Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zohary, D. &amp; Hopf, M. Domestication of plants in the old world: The origin and spread of cultivated plants in West Asia, Europe and the Nile Valley. 3rd edn. (Oxford University, New York, 2000)" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4420e524">9</a></sup>. There are two major centers of wild grapevine species diversity, North America and East Asia with 28 and 30 species, respectively<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Reisch, B. J. &amp; Pratt, C. in Fruit Breeding, Vine and Small Fruits, (eds Janick, J. &amp; More, J. N.) Ch. 4, Vol. 2 (John Wiley and Sons, Inc., 1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4420e529">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wan, Y. Z. et al. A phylogenetic analysis of the grape genus (Vitis L.) reveals broad reticulation and concurrent diversification during Neogene and Quaternary climate change. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 141 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4420e532">8</a></sup>. In the period following the retreat of the Wisconsin glaciation (~11–10,000 years ago), which radically altered the geography of central and eastern North America, it is likely that the receding glaciers and harsh conditions allowed the eastern North American grapevine species to expand their range north into more varied and marginal conditions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Wan, Y. Z. et al. A phylogenetic analysis of the grape genus (Vitis L.) reveals broad reticulation and concurrent diversification during Neogene and Quaternary climate change. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 141 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4420e536">8</a></sup>. In eastern North America, <i>Vitis riparia, V. labrusca, V. aestivalis</i>, and <i>V. cinerea</i> developed large overlapping distributions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Reisch, B. J. &amp; Pratt, C. in Fruit Breeding, Vine and Small Fruits, (eds Janick, J. &amp; More, J. N.) Ch. 4, Vol. 2 (John Wiley and Sons, Inc., 1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4420e546">1</a></sup>. Hybridization of these species is common in the wild; however, distinct geographic, topographic, and climatic conditions have maintained the species diversification<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Klein, L. L. et al. High-throughput sequencing data clarify evolutionary relationships among North American Vitis species and improve identification in USDA Vitis germplasm collection. Am. J. Bot. 105, 215–216 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4420e550">10</a></sup>. Many breeders have utilized the biotic and abiotic stress tolerance traits in these species to produce grapes that are sustainable for production in harsh climatic and biotic conditions, whereas <i>V. vinifera</i> would be killed outright or require extraordinary viticultural intervention to maintain production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Breeder, G. in Grapevine Breeding Programs for the Wine Industry (ed Reynolds, A.) 1st edn, Ch. 17 (Woodhead Publishing, 2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4420e558">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Luby, J. J. Utilization of Vitis riparia for the development of new wine varieties with resistance to disease and extreme cold. (eds Bouquet, A. &amp; Boursiquot, M. J.) VII international symposium on grapevine genetics and breeding; Acta Horticulturae 528, 487–490 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4420e561">12</a></sup>.</p><p>One of the most commonly used species in abiotic and biotic stress tolerance breeding, <i>V. riparia</i>, has the largest continental distribution of the North American species. Its range stretches, from Texas in the south to the Riding Mountains (Manitoba, Canada) in the North and from the Rocky Mountains to the East Coast<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Reisch, B. J. &amp; Pratt, C. in Fruit Breeding, Vine and Small Fruits, (eds Janick, J. &amp; More, J. N.) Ch. 4, Vol. 2 (John Wiley and Sons, Inc., 1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4420e571">1</a></sup>. <i>V. riparia</i> genotypes have been utilized extensively in rootstock and scion breeding for its freezing tolerance, disease resistance (powdery mildew, downy mildew, and, <i>botrytis</i>), and phylloxera resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Breeder, G. in Grapevine Breeding Programs for the Wine Industry (ed Reynolds, A.) 1st edn, Ch. 17 (Woodhead Publishing, 2015)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4420e581">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Luby, J. J. Utilization of Vitis riparia for the development of new wine varieties with resistance to disease and extreme cold. (eds Bouquet, A. &amp; Boursiquot, M. J.) VII international symposium on grapevine genetics and breeding; Acta Horticulturae 528, 487–490 (2000)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4420e581_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Luby, J. &amp; Fennell, A. Fruit breeding for the northern great plains at the University of Minnesota and South Dakota State University. HortSci. 41, 25–26 (2006)." href="#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4420e581_2">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Lowe, K. M. &amp; Walker, M. A. Genetic linkage map of the interspecific grape rootstock cross Ramsey (Vitis champinii) x Riparia Gloire (Vitis riparia). Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 1582–1592 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4420e584">14</a></sup>. <i>V. riparia</i> genotypes collected from the species’ northern range have been used to develop new cultivars. Presumably due to the capture of adaptive trait complexes that allow survival under harsh winter conditions. <i>V. riparia</i> based cultivars have contributed to the expansion of grapes in cold climate, resulting in over 2400 ha (hectares) of new production and 300 new wineries in the Northern United States and Southeastern Canada<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Breeder, G. in Grapevine Breeding Programs for the Wine Industry (ed Reynolds, A.) 1st edn, Ch. 17 (Woodhead Publishing, 2015)." href="#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4420e595">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Luby, J. J. Utilization of Vitis riparia for the development of new wine varieties with resistance to disease and extreme cold. (eds Bouquet, A. &amp; Boursiquot, M. J.) VII international symposium on grapevine genetics and breeding; Acta Horticulturae 528, 487–490 (2000)." href="#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4420e595_1">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Luby, J. &amp; Fennell, A. Fruit breeding for the northern great plains at the University of Minnesota and South Dakota State University. HortSci. 41, 25–26 (2006)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4420e598">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Tuck, B., Gartner, W. &amp; Appiah, G. Vineyards and grapes of the North. University of Minnesota. University of Minnesota Digital Conservancy, &#xA;http://hdl.handle.net/11299/197814&#xA;&#xA; (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4420e601">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Tuck, B., Gartner, W., Appiah, G. Wineries of the North. University of Minnesota. Retrieved from the University of Minnesota Digital Conservancy, &#xA;http://hdl.handle.net/11299/197823&#xA;&#xA; (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4420e604">16</a></sup>. Mining the locally adaptive traits from <i>V. riparia</i> has resulted in new cultivars that incorporate the traits of early ripening, high sugar content and maximum freezing tolerance traits<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Breeder, G. in Grapevine Breeding Programs for the Wine Industry (ed Reynolds, A.) 1st edn, Ch. 17 (Woodhead Publishing, 2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4420e611">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Hemstad, P. R. &amp; Luby, J. J. Utilization of Vitis riparia for the development of new wine varieties with resistance to disease and extreme cold. (eds Bouquet, A. &amp; Boursiquot, M. J.) VII international symposium on grapevine genetics and breeding; Acta Horticulturae 528, 487–490 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4420e614">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Fennell, A., Mathiason, K. &amp; Luby, J. Genetic segregation for indicators of photoperiod control of dormancy induction in Vitis species. Acta Hort. 689, 533 (2005). 539." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4420e617">17</a></sup>. While breeding new cultivars using <i>V. riparia</i> has been successful, there are large gaps in our understanding of the genetic architecture of positive and negative traits that this species brings into the breeding programs.</p><p>To fill this gap, the genome of <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ a genotype from the Riding Mountains of Manitoba, Canada was sequenced for assembly, single nucleotide polymorphism mapping, pan-generic marker panel development and genetic analysis. <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ was obtained from the breeding program at the University of Minnesota, St. Paul, MN, USA and later placed in the USDA ARS Germplasm Repository at Geneva, New York under the identifier of PI588259. The strategy undertaken here was to use the highly accurate Illumina short-read sequencing in conjunction with mate pair libraries to produce an assembly with high fidelity that matches the greater heterozygosity of the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ genome. This genotype has been used extensively in studies to develop improved cold hardiness, photoperiod responsiveness, identify the female allele of the <i>Vitis</i> sex locus, and break the linkage drag between high malic acid and soluble solids in segregating populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yang et al. Next generation mapping of enological traits in an F2 interspecific grapevine hybrid family. PLoS ONE 11, e0149560 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4420e639">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zou, C. et al. Haplotyping the Vitis collinear core genome with rhAmpSeq improves marker transferability in a diverse genus. Nat. Commun. 11, 413 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4420e642">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fennell, A., Mathiason, K. &amp; Luby, J. Genetic segregation for indicators of photoperiod control of dormancy induction in Vitis species. Acta Hort. 689, 533 (2005). 539." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4420e645">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garris, A. et al. Mapping of photoperiod-induced growth cessation in the wild grape Vitis riparia. J. Am. Soc. Hort. Sci. 134, 261–272 (2009)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4420e645_1">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Fennell, A., Alahakoon, D., Luby, J. &amp; Clark, M. Mapping the genetic architecture of grapevine bud growth-cycling quantitative traits. Acta Hort. 1248, 337–343 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4420e648">19</a></sup>. In addition to breeding, this genotype has also been used extensively for physiological, proteomic, transcriptomic, and metabolomic analyses and GBS mapping studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yang et al. Next generation mapping of enological traits in an F2 interspecific grapevine hybrid family. PLoS ONE 11, e0149560 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4420e653">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zou, C. et al. Haplotyping the Vitis collinear core genome with rhAmpSeq improves marker transferability in a diverse genus. Nat. Commun. 11, 413 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4420e656">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fennell, A. &amp; Line, M. J. Identifying differential tissue response in grape (Vitis riparia) during induction of endodormancy using nuclear magnetic resonance imaging. J. Am. Soc. Hort. Sci. 126, 681–688 (2001)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4420e659">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fennell, A. et al. Short day transcriptomic programming during induction of dormancy in grapevine. Front Plant Sci. 6, 834 (2015)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4420e659_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="George, I. S., Fennell, A. Y. &amp; Haynes, P. A. Protein identification and quantification from riverbank grape, Vitis riparia: Comparing SDS-PAGE and FASP-GPF techniques for shotgun proteomic analysis. Proteomics 16, 3061–3065 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4420e662">22</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><p>We present here, a detailed analysis of stress tolerant <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ genome assembly, using short-read Illumina data. First the genetic relationship of <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and USDA ARS Germplasm Repository <i>V. riparia</i> materials, originally collected throughout <i>V. riparia’s</i> native range, was examined using informative SNPs. Secondly, a de novo assembly was developed for this heterozygous species using paired-end short-read libraries totaling 369.7X coverage. Gene prediction was conducted using RNASeq data from multiple tissues and experiments. Finally, the final assembly and gene models were compared with the reference genome <i>V. vinifera</i> PN40024 12X.2 and V3 annotation and other recent <i>Vitis</i> cultivar assemblies and gene conservation was analyzed. In addition to validating the quality of the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ short-read assembly, we conducted analysis of the <i>WRKY</i> domain (<i>WRKY</i>), myelobsastosis (<i>MYB</i>), and ethylene response factor (<i>ERF</i>) transcription factor (TF) gene families and evaluated transposon composition in <i>V. riparia</i> versus <i>V. vinifera</i> genomes. Sequenced SNP markers from a F2 mapping population, identified by aligning genotype sequence data to <i>V. vinifera</i>, were used to test the fidelity and utility of this <i>V. riparia</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Yang et al. Next generation mapping of enological traits in an F2 interspecific grapevine hybrid family. PLoS ONE 11, e0149560 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4420e721">2</a></sup>. The SNP markers were aligned to the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and <i>V. vinifera</i> genomes and quantitative trait loci regions over genic locations were mapped to the genomes using <i>V. riparia</i> phenotypes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genetic analysis of natively collected <i>V. riparia</i>
</h3><p>The genetic relationship of <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ relative to other <i>V. riparia</i> in the USDA ARS Germplasm Repository, Geneva, NY USA indicated that ‘Manitoba 37’ is representative of the <i>V. riparia</i> collected throughout its native range (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig1">1a, b</a>; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM1">1</a>). Principal component analysis (PCA) of SNP data from 68 <i>V</i>. <i>riparia</i> samples demonstrated that <i>V. riparia</i> diversity is best described as two separate clusters. Both <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and the important rootstock cultivar <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Gloire de Montpellier’ are in the primary cluster, and a secondary cluster is made up of species from the Northwestern edge of <i>V. riparia’s</i> range (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig1">1a, b</a>). This split is not observed in PCA2vsPCA3 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM1">1</a>). Further differentiation between ‘Manitoba 37’ and ‘Gloire de Montpellier’ and the secondary cluster can be observed in PCA1vsPCA4 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig1">1a, b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="V. riparia genetic and assembly characterization."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: <i>V. riparia</i> genetic and assembly characterization.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0316-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_316_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0316-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_316_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="569" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <b>b</b> Principal component analysis of informative SNPs in 68 <i>V. riparia</i> individuals in the USDA ARS Geneva New York germplasm repository. Symbols represent sample origin by state (United States) and Canadian province as noted in the USDA Germplasm Resource Information Network. Open squares indicate individuals with unknown geographic origin. <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and <i>V. riparia</i>’Gloire de Montpellier’ (unknown geographic origin) are represented by blue diamond and black square, respectively. SNP data was derived from GBS data that was previously reported in Klein et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Klein, L. L. et al. High-throughput sequencing data clarify evolutionary relationships among North American Vitis species and improve identification in USDA Vitis germplasm collection. Am. J. Bot. 105, 215–216 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4420e814">10</a></sup>. <b>a</b> Principal components one and two, 10.6% and 5.9% of variation, respectively. <b>b</b> Presents principal components one and four, 10.6% and 4.5% of variation, respectively. <b>c</b> K-mer analysis of <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ genome. The 19 k-mer was carried out with 262.3X coverage by Jellyfish and heterozygosity obtained by GenomeScope. The first peak located at coverage 89X corresponds to the heterozygous and the second peak at 184X corresponds to the homozygous peak. The genome contains 1.39% heterozygosity (identified with 262.3X coverage). <b>d</b> Dot plot of global alignment between <i>V. vinifera</i> PN40024 on the <i>x</i>-axis and <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ on the <i>y</i>-axis</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">De novo heterozygous assembly and validation</h3><p>Using Illumina HiSeq (Illumina, USA) short reads and three mate-pair libraries of varying insert sizes, we generated 2295.4 M raw reads for the <i>V. riparia</i> diploid genome draft assembly. A 1.39% heterozygosity was estimated from the unprocessed short-reads with the Jellyfish plot showing the heterozygous peak slightly lower than the homozygous peak (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig1">1c</a>). The initial assembly had a N50 of 512,151 bp and 13.63% scaffold %N. After gap closing, the final <i>V. riparia</i> assembly was 494.6 Mb in 69,616 scaffolds, with an N50 of 518,740 bp and a scaffold N of 3.57% (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM6">1</a>). The <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and <i>V. vinifera</i> PN40024 alignment are shown as a dot plot (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig1">1d</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly and gene prediction statistics</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In addition to the Assemblathon statistics, 96% of the filtered reads mapped back to the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ genome assembly with zero mismatch. REAPR analysis of genome assembly accuracy using mate paired-end reads found evidence (low mate paired coverage) for potential mis-assembly in no more than 16% of the scaffolds (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM7">2a</a>). Mapping of EST and BAC sequences indicated that 1935 of 1974 (98%) ESTs and 3811 of 4171 (91.3%) of the BACs mapped to the assembled genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM7">2b</a>). Alignment of three different <i>V. riparia</i> de novo transcriptomes with the <i>V. riparia</i> genome assembly resulted in &gt;99% mapping of the total transcripts for each transcriptome, with &gt;93% transcripts mapping with &gt;90% identity and &gt;90% coverage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM7">2b</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Alignment of <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly with <i>V. vinifera</i> genomes</h3><p>The <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ de novo scaffolds aligned well with <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’. A total of 76% of the <i>V. vinifera</i> PN40024 scaffolds were aligned with &gt;90% identity and &gt;1000 bp identity and 9496 scaffolds had &gt;95% identity with &gt; 1000 bp alignment (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM2">2</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM8">3</a>). A total of 1,607,090 high quality SNPs were called with a rate of 1 every 504 bases (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM2">2</a>).</p><p>Similar alignment statistics were observed for other recent <i>Vitis</i> genomes. <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ scaffolds aligned with &gt;90% identity and &gt;1000 bp identity to primary-contig scaffolds of <i>V. vinifera</i> cultivars ‘Cabernet Sauvignon’, ‘Chardonnay’, and ‘Carménère’ and <i>V. riparia ‘</i>Gloire de Montpellier’ at 75%, 74%, 76.5%, and 90.6%, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM8">3</a>). A zero-mismatch filtered reads mapping found only 89% of the reads mapping to ‘Gloire de Montpellier’ assembly, in contrast to 96% mapping to the ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repeat identification and de novo gene prediction</h3><p>Repeat sequences were predicted to make up 46% of the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4a</a>). These repeats are predominantly LTR regions (17.68%), long interspersed nuclear elements (LINE) (4.21%), DNA elements (2.06%) and Unclassified repeats (19.95%) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4a</a>). The de novo gene prediction using <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ RNAseq data identified 40,019 putative coding sequences with the average size of predicted coding sequence (CDS) is 984.4 bp (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Tab1">1</a>). A total of 1548 (96%) Benchmarking Universal Single-copy Orthologs (BUSCO) were identified in the predicted genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4b</a>). Species distribution of BLASTX results showed most of the predicted <i>V. riparia</i> genes correspond with <i>V. vinifera</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4c</a>). Over 89% of coding sequences were fully annotated using the <i>V. vinifera ‘</i>PN40024’ 41,733 CDS. A total of 41,189 (99%) <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ coding sequences aligned to the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly. Of these 33,370 (80%) <i>V. vinifera</i> CDS had &gt;90% identity and &gt;70% coverage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM7">2b</a>) with <i>V. riparia</i> predicted gene models. In total, 5,596 enzymes were identified from the six enzyme classes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4d</a>). Gene ontology characterization of the <i>V. riparia</i> coding sequences of <i>V. riparia</i> functionally annotated the majority of the CDS (Biological Process (45,720), Cellular component (34,793), and Molecular Function (29,046)) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4e</a>). All coding sequences were queried against the InterPro database and 35,643 sequences were annotated with 20,382 coding sequences containing GO identifiers. Analysis of KEGG annotations resulted in 133 different pathways covering 1,233 coding sequences (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM9">4f</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Comparative genome analysis</h3><p>Synteny comparisons were made for <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’, <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ and <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘Sultanina’ and genome assemblies in the Fabidae and Malvidae orders of the superrosids (<i>Medicago trunculata</i>, and <i>Fragaria vesca</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> respectively). The <i>V. riparia</i> scaffolds arranged in pseudo-molecules were aligned with each plant genome separately (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM10">5a, c, d</a>). The greatest number of extended conserved syntenic blocks (&gt;10 kb) was observed with the reference genome <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ and then <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘Sultanina’ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig2">2a</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM10">5c</a>). <i>Medicago</i> had the highest number of syntenic blocks for the examined Rosid genomes, followed by <i>Populus</i> and <i>Fragaria</i>; the lowest synteny was observed with <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM10">5b, c</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Comparative analysis of V. riparia ‘Manitoba 37’ with other plant species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>A comparison of predicted <i>V. riparia</i> genes to multiple species was used to further characterize gene coverage. There were 13,964 common orthologous protein sequences shared by <i>V. riparia</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i> 12X.2 and <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘Sultanina’, and 9661 found in common with Rosid species (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Fragaria vesca</i> and <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig2">2b, c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM10">5d</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Plant transcription factor identification</h3><p>Plant transcription factor analysis indicated that the <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ assembly contained representatives of all the transcription factor gene families found in <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM11">6</a>). A total of 1,723 transcription factors from 58 families were identified in the <i>V. riparia</i> assembly. There were 67 <i>V. riparia WRKY</i> transcription factors annotated and compared phylogenetically to the <i>V. vinifera</i> WRKY and other species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM11">6</a>a–c; <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7a, b</a>). A putative novel <i>WRKY</i> domain (WVDTDKR) was identified in the <i>V. riparia</i> gene Vitri g36183.t1; however, this putative <i>WRKY</i> domain was not shared with any <i>WRKY</i> domain in <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7a–c</a>). There was a greater number of potential <i>MYB</i> family transcription factors identified in <i>V. riparia</i> (13 <i>MYB</i> genes and 35 <i>MYB</i>-related genes) than found in <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7d</a>). In the 25 <i>MYB</i> family subgroups, only subgroup 6 had potential gene duplications in comparison to <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM3">3</a>). Comparison of the <i>V. riparia</i> Ethylene Response Factor transcription factor (<i>ERF</i>) genes with the <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ <i>ERF</i> groupings indicate all groups were represented in <i>V. riparia;</i> however, there were fewer <i>ERF</i> identified in <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ than in <i>V. vinifera</i>. (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM4">4</a>; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7e</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="V. riparia ‘Manitoba 37’ and V. vinifera ‘PN40024’ WRKY transcription factors."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ and <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ <i>WRKY</i> transcription factors.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0316-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_316_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0316-2/MediaObjects/41438_2020_316_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="549" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Phylogenetic relationship of <i>WRKY</i> TFs between the two species annotated by conserved group. Red dots represent <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ genes and blue dots represent <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’ genes. Bootstrap values displayed are at nodes. Gene id and group identity are found in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7b</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Analysis of protein families from <i>V. riparia</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>
</h3><p>The predicted <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘PN40024’, <i>V. riparia ‘</i>Manitoba 37’, and <i>V. vinifera</i> ‘Sultanina’ genes were aligned to the protein family database (PFAM). The <i>V. riparia</i> and the seedless cultivar ‘Sultanina’ had a lower copy number of predicted genes related to transposases and transposons than <i>V. vinifera</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig4">4a</a>). These results are supported by annotation to GO terms for each family (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig4">4b</a>). A closer look at the <i>RETROTRANS_GAG</i> 2 (<i>LTR</i>) transposon family shows paralogous duplication of genes in each clade that most likely occurred after the species diverged geographically (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig4">4c</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7f</a>). A comparison of <i>LATERAL ORGAN BOUNDARY DOMAIN</i> containing proteins (<i>LBD</i>) in <i>V. vinifera</i> and <i>V. riparia</i> ‘Manitoba 37’ found high conservation similarities between members of each subfamily from both genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#Fig4">4d</a>, Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0316-2#MOESM12">7g</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of protein families in V. riparia and V. vinifera."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Analysis of protein families in <i>V. riparia</i> and <i>V. vinifera</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Heatmap displaying the differences in numbers of genes per <b>a</b> protein family or <b>b</b> Gene ontology (GO) term. <b>c</b> Protein alignment of members of the “retrotrans_gag 2” family (<i>LTR</i> retrotransposons).
